<div id="review_12102163" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/12102163" />
    <a title="Derrick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/27573-derrick"><img alt="Derrick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1222963006p2/27573.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/12102163?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Derrick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Derrick" href="/user/show/27573-derrick">Derrick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743457903" href="/book/show/18989.Tinker_Tailor_Soldier_Spy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer12102163" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15121507576774816522">Oft billed as the "anti-Ian Flemming," John Le Carre inverts all the typical trappings of the spy-thriller: in place of the handsome, gadget-happy g-man we're given a sacked, middle-aged cuckold whose attention to detail and intellectual virtuosity quietly derail Moscow Central's invisible vise-grip on the Circus.<br/><br/>Note that "quietly," as the tension here is all cerebral, the violence and spectacle off-stage, and the stakes themselves, though no less dire than the fate of the world, are entirely i</span>
  <span id="freeText15121507576774816522" style="display:none">Oft billed as the "anti-Ian Flemming," John Le Carre inverts all the typical trappings of the spy-thriller: in place of the handsome, gadget-happy g-man we're given a sacked, middle-aged cuckold whose attention to detail and intellectual virtuosity quietly derail Moscow Central's invisible vise-grip on the Circus.<br /><br />Note that "quietly," as the tension here is all cerebral, the violence and spectacle off-stage, and the stakes themselves, though no less dire than the fate of the world, are entirely ideological.<br /><br />The Cold War assurance of mutual destruction provides the British imagination with a field of conflict perfectly tailored to the restriction of overt or "hot" action (Smiley's also impotent), which is then carefully sublimated through elaborately mannered, gentlemanly games of intelligence and subterfuge. <br /><br />Himself a former blown secret agent for MI6, Le Carre writes with all the authority and flare one would expect from a once genuine article, though without all the lurid technical gun-fetishism of a Tom Clancy or Ian Flemming. A great deal of the prose is composed of contextually self-evident turns of phrase that seems to have bucked a number of readers at this site--while not jargon, this writing style suggests a world behind the world more interested in demonstrating, rather than explaining, itself. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15121507576774816522" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2729665550" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2729665550" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2729665550?book_show_action=true">Mar 02, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/6899593-tinker-tailor-soldier-spy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=cold-war">cold-war</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=spy-thriller">spy-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2729665550" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18110526742942587508"><b>
  <i>”I like you to have doubts,” he said. “It tells me where you stand. But don’t make a cult of them or you’ll be a bore.”</i>
</b><br/><br/>Impossible to think how anyone could live the life of one of John le Carre’s cold war spies and not be assaulted by doubt day and night. Still, they must all be entertaining just the right amount, because none of these characters is a bore. Nobody does spy thriller quite as well as John le Carre, and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10073506.Tinker__Tailor__Soldier__Spy__The_Karla_Trilogy__1_' title='Tinker, Tailor, Soldier, Spy (The Karla Trilogy #1) by John le Carré' rel='nofollow noopener'>Tinker, Tailor, Soldier, Spy</a> is John le Carre at his best. <br/><br/>Of course, there</span>
  <span id="freeText18110526742942587508" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>”I like you to have doubts,” he said. “It tells me where you stand. But don’t make a cult of them or you’ll be a bore.”</i>
</b><br /><br />Impossible to think how anyone could live the life of one of John le Carre’s cold war spies and not be assaulted by doubt day and night. Still, they must all be entertaining just the right amount, because none of these characters is a bore. Nobody does spy thriller quite as well as John le Carre, and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/10073506.Tinker__Tailor__Soldier__Spy__The_Karla_Trilogy__1_" title="Tinker, Tailor, Soldier, Spy (The Karla Trilogy #1) by John le Carré" rel="nofollow noopener">Tinker, Tailor, Soldier, Spy</a> is John le Carre at his best. <br /><br />Of course, there are the usual elements of a spy novel. The Russians are pitted against the Western world and, in this particular case, there is a mole within the Circus that is wreaking havoc on the entire system. Although he has been ousted from the service, George Smiley is called in to sort through the detritus and unravel the gordian knot, and the strands seem to run in all directions right up to the end. Ah, but there is so much more than that.<br /><br />George Smiley is such a unique character. Like Sherlock Holmes, he has a personae all his own; unlike Holmes, he is an unassuming, all too human, observer of humanity. While most people, particularly those at the Circus, are scrambling to reach the top and be in control, Smiley seems to accept the role of power thrust upon him rather reluctantly. <br /><br /><i>He was aware of a modest sense of approaching conquest. He had been driven a long way, he had sailed backwards and forwards. Tomorrow, if he was lucky, he might spot land; a peaceful little desert island, for instance. Somewhere Karla had never heard of. Just for him and Ann.</i><br /><br />I believe it makes him more likeable that his dreams are as unlikely to come true as any we might dream ourselves. He sees into the hearts of other people, a deft listener who understands what is going on beneath the surface; but he is often blind to himself, and aren’t we all.<br /><br />This is a novel about spies, but it is also a novel about intrigue, friendship, deception, idol worship, and betrayal. Betrayal in all its forms, betrayal of country, of friendship, of love, of innocence, and of trust. Perhaps men in this line of work should not expect better, but we come to understand that these men are the “lovely boys” who came into this darkness after fighting a war on the side of morality and when still charged with the ideals of that war and their youth. The men they become bear almost no resemblance to the boys they were, and the reality of what they have become is heartbreaking. <br /><br />This was not my first reading of this remarkable book, and I find it lost none of its luster during all those years it was collecting dust on my bookshelf.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="18110526742942587508" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2552329249" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2552329249" />
    <a title="Whispering Stories" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/20354973-whispering-stories"><img alt="Whispering Stories" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1548065529p2/20354973.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2552329249?book_show_action=true">Oct 05, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Whispering Stories" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Whispering Stories" href="/user/show/20354973-whispering-stories">Whispering Stories</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2552329249" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14077821497329309190">Book Reviewed by Stacey on <a href='https://www.whisperingstories.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>www.whisperingstories.com</a><br/><br/>This is the first John Le Carré book that I have ever read. When I mentioned it to a few people that I would be reading it they all said the same thing, ‘it is a great book to get me introduced to the author’s work’. I will admit that I know of the book and that a few years ago there was a BBC adaptation of it and a recent movie. Alas, I have seen none, so apart from the synopsis and little snippets people were telling me, I knew nothing about </span>
  <span id="freeText14077821497329309190" style="display:none">Book Reviewed by Stacey on <a href="https://www.whisperingstories.com" rel="nofollow noopener">www.whisperingstories.com</a><br /><br />This is the first John Le Carré book that I have ever read. When I mentioned it to a few people that I would be reading it they all said the same thing, ‘it is a great book to get me introduced to the author’s work’. I will admit that I know of the book and that a few years ago there was a BBC adaptation of it and a recent movie. Alas, I have seen none, so apart from the synopsis and little snippets people were telling me, I knew nothing about the book.<br /><br />My first impressions of Le Carré’s work is how rich in vocabulary it is, you can certainly tell it was written a few decades ago from the carefully chosen descriptive words. Whilst this is a spy story set during the Cold War, there is no James Bond bling to it.<br /><br />George Smiley is a fascinating character. He has been brought back from retirement (this is the fifth George Smiley book), and give the task of searching for a mole high up in the ranks of the MI6. As well as George there is a vast array of characters, too many to mention and at times I did feel a little confused as to who was who. This is most definitely a book you need to concentrate on.<br /><br />It did take me a while to read and at times I re-read pages just to make sure I had the story straight. I feel this is more down to the fact I rarely read older books, so the language and the tone took some getting used to.<br /><br />Overall I found the book absorbing. I was expecting the plot to jump right into the thick of it, so I was a little confused by the opening scenes in a school, but everything came together nicely.<br /><br />I have now been compelled to read more by John Le Carré, including ‘The Little Drummer Girl’, though I might watch the BBC adaptation coming soon first this time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14077821497329309190" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=summer-of-spies">summer-of-spies</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=espionage">espionage</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=read-in-2018">read-in-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=london">london</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=z-1001-books">z-1001-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/9246169-wanda?shelf=public-library">public-library</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2124728364" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7569297298712886730"><b>
  <i>***2018 Summer of Spies***</i>
</b><br/><br/>Well, you couldn’t get much further away from the playboy-spy image than this, could you? George Smiley, the chubby everyman who’s always polishing his glasses, is the antithesis of James Bond. Rather than Miss Moneypenny, there’s a whole department of women known as “the mothers.” And instead of posh casinos, George spends a lot of time in a run-down hotel, reading swathes of paper files.<br/><br/>This is spy work done through the archives, searching for patterns in the pape</span>
  <span id="freeText7569297298712886730" style="display:none"><b>
  <i>***2018 Summer of Spies***</i>
</b><br /><br />Well, you couldn’t get much further away from the playboy-spy image than this, could you? George Smiley, the chubby everyman who’s always polishing his glasses, is the antithesis of James Bond. Rather than Miss Moneypenny, there’s a whole department of women known as “the mothers.” And instead of posh casinos, George spends a lot of time in a run-down hotel, reading swathes of paper files.<br /><br />This is spy work done through the archives, searching for patterns in the paperwork, and through careful interviews with those who have been betrayed and/or let go. We have hints that Smiley had his daring days when he was younger, but he’s now a middle-aged man using his intellect instead of his muscles, carefully piecing together the story. Sometimes he learns as much from what’s not said as from what is said. Plus, he’s reinvigorating his career—sacked because he sided with the wrong person (Control), he is getting his place in the biz back by figuring out which high-level Intelligence man is their Russian mole. <br /><br />Double agents, backstabbing, and betrayal. What more can you ask for in a novel?<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7569297298712886730" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer9714318898672733011">First off, I understand that <i>Tinker Tailor</i> is a spy novel, and that Le Carre obviously wanted to achieve a certain effect appropriate to the genre, and to keep everything "realistic." But it was jargon-y to a fault, and in keeping its audience as in the dark as its protagonist, it succeeded too well.<br/><br/>Furthermore, its characters never spoke the way they were described - it was always "'could you pass the tea please, that's a boy,' he shouted furiously." And about 95% of the book is written in past</span>
  <span id="freeText9714318898672733011" style="display:none">First off, I understand that <i>Tinker Tailor</i> is a spy novel, and that Le Carre obviously wanted to achieve a certain effect appropriate to the genre, and to keep everything "realistic." But it was jargon-y to a fault, and in keeping its audience as in the dark as its protagonist, it succeeded too well.<br /><br />Furthermore, its characters never spoke the way they were described - it was always "'could you pass the tea please, that's a boy,' he shouted furiously." And about 95% of the book is written in past perfect tense, which I had had (!) about enough of after two chapters.<br /><br />So someone needs to tell me how the Smiley books got so popular. Are the rest better?</span>

<div id="review_3291548293" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3291548293" />
    <a title="Barbara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/93114967-barbara"><img alt="Barbara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1551265103p2/93114967.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3291548293?book_show_action=true">Aug 03, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Barbara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Barbara" href="/user/show/93114967-barbara">Barbara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781433261176" href="/book/show/7284146-tinker-tailor-soldier-spy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/93114967-barbara?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/93114967-barbara?shelf=best-of-2020">best-of-2020</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/93114967-barbara?shelf=spy-thriller">spy-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/93114967-barbara?shelf=smiley">smiley</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3291548293" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6596046480587064850">Oh, the joys of reading the master of the understated spy thriller! To quote a theme song from a movie based on a book by a very different spy novelist, "Nobody Does It Better". <br/><br/>Here we have Smiley fully developed as a character, out of the shadows, front and center in the plot. Not only do we observe Smiley's cautious unveiling of the mole at the very top levels of The Circus, the British spy agency, but we meet for the first time Russian spymaster Karla who will become his nemesis through two </span>
  <span id="freeText6596046480587064850" style="display:none">Oh, the joys of reading the master of the understated spy thriller! To quote a theme song from a movie based on a book by a very different spy novelist, "Nobody Does It Better". <br /><br />Here we have Smiley fully developed as a character, out of the shadows, front and center in the plot. Not only do we observe Smiley's cautious unveiling of the mole at the very top levels of The Circus, the British spy agency, but we meet for the first time Russian spymaster Karla who will become his nemesis through two succeeding volumes. <br /><br />These books bear close reading - or listening, in my case. Nuances of plot and character are presented quietly as the story unfolds, the tone a reflection, perhaps, of Smiley's diffident personality. le Carré has Smiley weave together, through the carefully elicited recollections of others, the bits and pieces of an ill-fated venture which led to his own downfall (though he had no awareness of it at the time it occurred). We must pay careful attention to the subtext of each of Smiley's discoveries as they are related if we are to keep up with the subtleties of his mind.<br /><br />Each of the individuals who populate Smiley's world of spies is richly drawn. No character placeholders here, these are people with depth of personality, even if they aren't on the stage for long. This is essential since often it is the relationships among these characters that determine the events and the outcome of the story. <br /><br />Such a <b>
  <u>satisfying</u>
</b> read. BTW, I haven't yet acquired an audio copy of the next Smiley, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18990.The_Honourable_Schoolboy" title="The Honourable Schoolboy by John le Carré" rel="nofollow noopener">The Honourable Schoolboy</a>, but I hope I can find one with the same narrator. Frederick Davidson was superb.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6596046480587064850" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_474427828" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/474427828" />
    <a title="LeeAnne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4058534-leeanne"><img alt="LeeAnne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1547214305p2/4058534.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/474427828?book_show_action=true">Dec 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="LeeAnne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="LeeAnne" href="/user/show/4058534-leeanne">LeeAnne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743457903" href="/book/show/18989.Tinker_Tailor_Soldier_Spy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4058534-leeanne?shelf=germany">germany</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4058534-leeanne?shelf=literary-fic">literary-fic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4058534-leeanne?shelf=cold-war">cold-war</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer474427828" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12327020135071936905"><b>John Le Carre</b> <br/><br/> <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1407166686i/10649556.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Lot's of people whose taste I respect love John le Carré. Lots of very smart, well read people that I know absolutely love John le Carré. I just don't get it and I probably never will. <br/><br/>First, I find John le Carré books to be so slow. The action has almost always already taken place, so the reader learns about the action in retrospect, through vague “spy dialogue” between upper class old men who speak in riddles. For me, this slows down and dulls any action to a snails pace. <br/><br/>But, t</span>
  <span id="freeText12327020135071936905" style="display:none"><b>John Le Carre</b> <br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1407166686i/10649556.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Lot's of people whose taste I respect love John le Carré. Lots of very smart, well read people that I know absolutely love John le Carré. I just don't get it and I probably never will. <br><br>First, I find John le Carré books to be so slow. The action has almost always already taken place, so the reader learns about the action in retrospect, through vague “spy dialogue” between upper class old men who speak in riddles. For me, this slows down and dulls any action to a snails pace. <br><br>But, the most frustrating aspect of John le Carré books for me that is writing is purposely convoluted and obscure for one reason -- to confuse everything and anything that is happening so.... it all seems much more enigmatic than it actually is. <br><br>There tons of characters that pop in and out, these characters are never properly introduced to the reader, they are never explained, so you, the reader, must figure out who they are, what role they play in the story and why they are important. Again, this is done to make everything much more mysterious than it really is. <br><br>The plot is also always purposely ambiguous, muddy and nonsensical to... you guessed it, make the story seem much more mysterious than it really is. <br><br>My problem is, I don't find any of this mysterious. I just find it all extremely annoying and boring. When the audience doesn't know something, it is a mystery. When the author doesn't know something, it's ambiguously annoying and that’s not a good thing. <br><br> <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1407166686i/10649557.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>It might help to describe it like this: each sentence below has more than one meaning. You, the reader, must decide which meaning to apply to figure out the mystery of the story. <br><br>•	The lady hit the man with an umbrella.<br>•	He gave her cat food.<br>•	John went to the bank.<br>•	The man saw the boy with the binoculars.<br><br>I will not tell you which meaning I am using in my story, so it will be very hard to follow, making it all very mysterious. Or if you are like me, annoying. <br><br><br><br><br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="12327020135071936905" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_258513675" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/258513675" />
    <a title="El" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/83144-el"><img alt="El" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1479156984p2/83144.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/258513675?book_show_action=true">Jan 08, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="El" class="user" itemprop="url" name="El" href="/user/show/83144-el">El</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743457903" href="/book/show/18989.Tinker_Tailor_Soldier_Spy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=20th-centurylit-late">20th-centurylit-late</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=wanderlust">wanderlust</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83144-el?shelf=eastern-central-european-lit">eastern-central-european-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer258513675" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6782338321946542127">Apparently I'm turning into a really shitty reader.<br/><br/>This is the first Le Carré I've read, and whatever, I think I expected a little more James Bond than, well, George Smiley - a name which every single time was mentioned (which was quite a lot) always made me think of his <a href='http://images.wikia.com/muppet/images/7/79/GuySmiley-2009.jpg' rel='nofollow noopener'>muppet-brother, Guy Smiley</a>. Picturing <i>George</i> as a human and not a muppet made the reading more difficult than I had intended. (See first note about becoming a really shitty reader.)<br/><br/>And in my current mood I wanted some violence. </span>
  <span id="freeText6782338321946542127" style="display:none">Apparently I'm turning into a really shitty reader.<br /><br />This is the first Le Carré I've read, and whatever, I think I expected a little more James Bond than, well, George Smiley - a name which every single time was mentioned (which was quite a lot) always made me think of his <a href="http://images.wikia.com/muppet/images/7/79/GuySmiley-2009.jpg" rel="nofollow noopener">muppet-brother, Guy Smiley</a>. Picturing <i>George</i> as a human and not a muppet made the reading more difficult than I had intended. (See first note about becoming a really shitty reader.)<br /><br />And in my current mood I wanted some violence. And by "violence" I mean some freaking action. Car chases, sword-play, maybe some poison or something. In other words, I wanted a story that <i>went somewhere</i>.<br /><br />This is not that book.<br /><br />What this book has is puzzles! Which is all fine and dandy, but gracious, if I was in the mood for puzzles I'd pull out my crosswords. The one time in my life I'm not in the mood to figure out a puzzle and this is the book I choose to read. Sorry, Mr. Le Carré, don't take it personally.<br /><br />I'm not ashamed to say that I read this primarily because there's a new movie version out with my celebrity sugar-daddy in it, Gary Oldman. He's decidedly not at all a muppet, and I have to say that the previews look pretty exciting. But that's Hollywood for you.<br /><br />This is a worthless review. Whatever, you should read it, I'm sure you'll like it. I for one am still excited to see the movie. If the first half of the movie is as slow as the first half of the book, though, I'll require extra popcorn to make up for it.<br /><br />Note: I'm including this on my Eastern-Central-European-Lit shelf because of all the talky-talk of Prague, the land of my people. Actually that made me just bump this book up to a 3-star read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6782338321946542127" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_228217959" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/228217959" />
    <a title="Ryan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1500718-ryan"><img alt="Ryan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1238846167p2/1500718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/228217959?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ryan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ryan" href="/user/show/1500718-ryan">Ryan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780340993767" href="/book/show/7081540-tinker-tailor-soldier-spy">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer228217959" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2350670870730523225">Veterans of Britain's secret service refer to MI6 as the Circus, and when <i>Tinker Tailor Soldier Spy</i> opens, our hero George Smiley has been kicked out of the show. So Smiley has not retired with dignity, but rather has been ousted for backing a jaded horse. The head of Circus, a spy so skilled that people only knew him as "Control," went out in a blaze of tragedy, and Smiley's career was one of the casualties.<br/><br/>Unknown to most, Control was trying to find a mole. He failed and the Circus has been re</span>
  <span id="freeText2350670870730523225" style="display:none">Veterans of Britain's secret service refer to MI6 as the Circus, and when <i>Tinker Tailor Soldier Spy</i> opens, our hero George Smiley has been kicked out of the show. So Smiley has not retired with dignity, but rather has been ousted for backing a jaded horse. The head of Circus, a spy so skilled that people only knew him as "Control," went out in a blaze of tragedy, and Smiley's career was one of the casualties.<br /><br />Unknown to most, Control was trying to find a mole. He failed and the Circus has been reorganized. Now, however, one of the Circus's spies surfaces with a suspicious tale of betrayal. In Hong Kong, he has heard rumors of a mole placed right at the top of the Circus. So who watches the watchmen?<br /><br />George Smiley does.<br /><br />As far as spies go, George Smiley is as far removed from James Bond and Jason Bourne as we can imagine. For one thing, George is over the hill. For another, George's wife, Ann, has cheated on him many times. In fact, one character that slept with Ann explains that he just joined the "queue." George Smiley is a man whose most distinguishing feature may well be his glasses.<br /><br />Which is another way of saying that we're invited to consider what's going on inside of Smiley's head -- and he's a master spy, so consider carefully. I quite like the way that le Carre illustrates Smiley's inner world. One early description of Smiley, who is struggling not to rot in his retirement, caught my eye:<br /><br /><blockquote>He would set up as a mild eccentric, discursive, withdrawn, but possessing one or two lovable habits such as muttering to himself while he bumbled along pavements. Out of date, perhaps, but who wasn’t these days? Out of date, but loyal to his own time. At a certain moment, after all, every man chooses: will he go forward, will he go back? There was nothing dishonorable in not being blown about by every little modern wind. Better to have worth, to entrench, to be an oak of one’s own generation</blockquote><br />Well said, though this is a message that 21st century readers might struggle to understand. "'Not being blown about by every little modern wind?' Huh?"<br /><br />In many ways, <i>Tinker Tailor Soldier Spy</i> has the feel of a novel that was written in a past age. Its atmosphere is dominated by the bitter paranoia of the Cold War, spies rely on tapes with tape in them to record incriminating evidence, and files are made out of printed paper that was typed by a human being using a typewriter. This is a world in which the English Empire still exists within the living memory of England's citizens, as opposed to dusty archives of post-colonial libraries.<br /><br /><i>Tinker Tailor Soldier Spy</i> is not a novel for readers seeking the action-adventurer. Instead, this is a book for readers interested in an unusual spy novel. Le Carre discusses aging, betrayal, and resilience, as opposed to coming of age, vengeance, and persistence.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2350670870730523225" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_297557877" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/297557877" />
    <a title="Trevor" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/175635-trevor"><img alt="Trevor" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1565151545p2/175635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/297557877?book_show_action=true">Mar 20, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Trevor" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Trevor" href="/user/show/175635-trevor">Trevor</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780743457903" href="/book/show/18989.Tinker_Tailor_Soldier_Spy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/175635-trevor?shelf=literature">literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/175635-trevor?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer297557877" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8415012012656802686">I caught up with a friend a few weeks ago when I had just started this book and he said he had given up on the film and drifted off to sleep as he had completely lost track of what was going on and in the end couldn’t care either way anyway. That was exactly the experience I was having with the book and had thought it was just me. But then, all of a sudden, at about the middle of this one (I imagine just as George was dozing off in the film), the pieces of the jigsaw start dropping into place an</span>
  <span id="freeText8415012012656802686" style="display:none">I caught up with a friend a few weeks ago when I had just started this book and he said he had given up on the film and drifted off to sleep as he had completely lost track of what was going on and in the end couldn’t care either way anyway. That was exactly the experience I was having with the book and had thought it was just me. But then, all of a sudden, at about the middle of this one (I imagine just as George was dozing off in the film), the pieces of the jigsaw start dropping into place and it all starts making sense. This still isn’t my favourite of his novels (I think maybe The Constant Gardner, Mission Song or Absolute Friends would have to fight it out for that honour – I envisage a three way arm-wrestle), but this was good enough to keep me interested, even though I knew, more or less, how it was going to turn out from about as soon as it started making sense. This is less of a mystery and more of a psychological drama, so knowing who did it doesn’t really matter too much. Not that I’m going to tell you, like. You need to keep your wits about you with this one - and no dozing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8415012012656802686" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/353521908" />
    <a title="Feliks" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5085729-feliks"><img alt="Feliks" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1445015906p2/5085729.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/353521908?book_show_action=true">Jun 22, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Feliks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Feliks" href="/user/show/5085729-feliks">Feliks</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553088441" href="/book/show/8219250-tinker-tailor-soldier-spy">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5085729-feliks?shelf=genre-thrillers">genre-thrillers</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer353521908" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11704446458098836517">For quite some time, this was one of the most amazing successes in the genre of espionage fiction. It reined supreme. The reading public had never seen anything quite like it. Everyone knew John LeCarre was a spy writer and that he was 'rather good'. Everyone--absolutely everyone--was aware of the landmark, the juggernaut which he had already achieved some years previously: <i>'The Spy Who Came in From the Cold'.</i> But no one --I think--expected him to equal that triumph; no one expected him to follo</span>
  <span id="freeText11704446458098836517" style="display:none">For quite some time, this was one of the most amazing successes in the genre of espionage fiction. It reined supreme. The reading public had never seen anything quite like it. Everyone knew John LeCarre was a spy writer and that he was 'rather good'. Everyone--absolutely everyone--was aware of the landmark, the juggernaut which he had already achieved some years previously: <i>'The Spy Who Came in From the Cold'.</i> But no one --I think--expected him to equal that triumph; no one expected him to follow that book with anything else as noteworthy. <em>'Cold'</em> had made the spy genre newly-impeccable; it brought espionage fiction up to the caliber of any fiction being written anywhere in the British-speaking world. It was a tremendous feat. <br /><br />And then there were a few years which went by... and then LeCarre comes up with this amazing book. Most people were probably only vaguely aware that his books were a continuing series; with an established set of re-appearing characters. But they really didn't know what this meant; they really didn't know what LeCarre was truly capable of until <em>this title </em>hit the market; and then everyone got it. It suddenly became clear really, what a spy novel could do; what a spy novel could encompass. LeCarre--in really a masterful, quiet, devoted manner--really crafted something <i>special</i> here. For the first time (I believe) readers were treated to something quite new. <br /><br />'Tinker, Tailor' continues the dramatic arc of a narrative premise which LeCarre started writing more than 10 years prior; when he created the 'George Smiley' hero; when he created some of the other figures in his fictional world of British espionage services. The plot of 'Tinker' reaches backwards in time and brings those characters forward...and really, this is the heart of LeCarre's technique. For, LeCarre's characters are 'just-like-us'; in that their lives span out across events and histories. Each 'Tinker' character has a rich interior life, extending backwards into the past, fanning out behind them. <br /><br />The novel wends its way among a jumble of poignant little mnemonic fragments which is tied to each figure drawn by LeCarre; fragments maybe mentioned only briefly in a completely previous LeCarre work, from years ago. Half-remembered allusions; scraps of near-forgotten admissions or confessions, confidences: this is how the Smiley-book plots must always resolve themselves. Never with a daring escape or assassination; always by 'reflection'. Ultimately, all LeCarre books are based on a trail of breadcrumbs dropped from the previous sequence of ...all his previous books. LeCarre makes new use of these crusts with every new outing. They become the caked, muddy foreground for each new mystery which Smiley must solve, as he must do here in 'Tinker'. <br /><br />So--for the reader--there's this wonderful redolence from seeing faintly-familiar names re-mentioned; hearing characters discuss their old history with each other. This is just as it happens in real life, and in real relationships. LeCarre-characters have palpable memories; they are always 'reaching back'; re-tracing their steps; re-playing past decisions and accidents and turning-points and gestures from LeCarre books which you last read perhaps five years earlier .<br /><br />Really, by the time 'Tinker' was released, reading leCarre novels had become a kind of 'collaborative pursuit' that each reader participated in alongside LeCarre and Smiley--a sort of investment on a personal level--purely from having read all those previous LeCarre romps. <br /><br />No other genre-author had the creativity to provide this sense of 'shared history' for readers; the built-up residuum of life which leCarre's introspective hero--George Smiley--must sift through as he goes about his discouraging investigatory task. Smiley must research the history of his friendships in this yarn; all his colleagues; his marriage; his friends. Basically, there is a Soviet mole in his department--the most devious mole ever--and it must be discovered whom--and the only way to do it, is with Smiley's particular brand of soul-searching. <br /><br />The materiele for that introspection is what we know well and care about fondly (if we've been reading Le Carre at all). George Smiley is an old friend, almost 'family'. We join in Smiley's urgent, private hunt for that suspected, hidden figure, the alleged burrower who--if he exists--has manipulated Smiley's life and his career for years. As Smiley burns; we burn.<br /><br />So, we enter into the 'Tinker' plot with an eagerness such as few stories of any kind in modern British lit, can invoke. For--when Smiley catches his enemy--he will solve in one stroke both a national problem and a personal problem. Smiley has been wounded on two levels by this mystery-figure (both 'officially' and 'inwardly').<br /><br />['Tinker' thus reminds us of one reason why espionage-fiction is the most literary of any genre. It reflects our concerns for our outward, external, social institutions and also treats the needs of our inward self; the psychology of the isolated citizen. In this way, LeCarre's writing is somewhat heir to fiction like that of Dostoevsky: he deals with 'man vs man' and also, 'man vs state'.]<br /><br />Back to my discourse: the 'inward' character-mining technique of LeCarre's came out previously in <em>"Cold"</em>. The trial of poor Alec Leamas in East Germany hinged on a scrap of circumstance mentioned one installment earlier in <i>'Call for the Dead'</i>--the cryptic 'business trip' by Hans-Dieter Mundt. <br /><br />But here, in 'Tinker' this 'back-pedaling of memory' is exercised even more vigorously. Why are leCarre plots always recounted via retrospect? Because the elusive quarry is never <em>'one of them'</em>. Smiley realizes (to his grief) that the man is <u>'one of us'.</u> He's not some wily, slippery, dirty foreigner in some distant land which our agents must go out and destroy in 'James Bond'-fashion. <br /><br />Indeed not. The evildoer, the enemy, the culprit in 'Tinker' is ..one of the members <em>of our own establishment.</em> And in order to unmask that infiltrator, Smiley has to ruminate upon whole swathes of British culture and society. [In a sense, this is why any 'agency mole' story is so rewarding. It forces (as no other genre trope does) a session of self-study; forces you to gaze at your own society to spot all the covered-up falsehoods.]<br /><br />Perhaps only in Dickens' novels do impassioned characters and their schemes cross lengthy intervals and lifetimes--do such 'feeling' agendas appear. LeCarre's semi-Dickensian technique radiated a new depth to the traditional spy story in a way that pays faith entirely to itself--to LeCarre himself (as an author, a citizen, and a man) and to his genre of choice. <br /><br />The resulting novel is a savory reading experience which satisfies fully well on its own, but--as a further laurel to the career of the man who had just written, <em>'In from the Cold'</em>--well, its really just <i>astounding.</i> In two swift strokes, John LeCarre gave us the definitive 'defector' novel and the definitive 'mole' novel. Think about how singular that is; and understand why no one can touch LeCarre in his chosen genre.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11704446458098836517" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name

    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('9xezRvbuGsCZuQUsi96IfgTeUhZBqXr+vEVn5SnMqMPNMdqUTbDTqD6SsU18K4Kh0oG13o8k9JnnOyFU5I9aWQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('cFh6QeFOteXv6rxnJkE9zt7wGUaN+uJgeADvT4pL+6RKfhOTWhB8jUjBCAbRtDcRCK/+jkN3bAcjfqn+RwgJPg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12987391570728868857">John le Carré, the pseudonym of David John Moore Cornwell (born 19 October 1931 in Poole, Dorset, England), is an English author of espionage novels. Le Carré has resided in St Buryan, Cornwall, Great Britain, for more than 40 years, where he owns a mile of cliff close to Land's End.<br/><br/>See also: <a href='https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_le_Carr%C3%A9' rel='nofollow noopener'>John le Carré - Wikipedia</a></span>
  <span id="freeText12987391570728868857" style="display:none">John le Carré, the pseudonym of David John Moore Cornwell (born 19 October 1931 in Poole, Dorset, England), is an English author of espionage novels. Le Carré has resided in St Buryan, Cornwall, Great Britain, for more than 40 years, where he owns a mile of cliff close to Land's End.<br /><br />See also: <a href="https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_le_Carr%C3%A9" rel="nofollow noopener">John le Carré - Wikipedia</a></span>
